<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">J-pop</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">

<p><b>J-pop</b> (often stylized as <b>J-POP</b>; <a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese</a>: <span lang="ja">ジェイポップ</span> <i>jeipoppu</i>; an abbreviation for <b>Japanese pop</b>), natively also known simply as <b>pops</b>, is a musical genre that entered the musical mainstream of <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> in the 1990s. Modern J-pop has its roots in traditional Japanese music, but significantly in <a href="/wiki/1960s_in_music" title="1960s in music">1960s</a> <a href="/wiki/Pop_music" title="Pop music">pop</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rock_music" title="Rock music">rock music</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/The_Beatles" title="The Beatles">The Beatles</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Beach_Boys" title="The Beach Boys">The Beach Boys</a>, which led to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japanese_rock" title="Japanese rock">Japanese rock</a> bands such as <a href="/wiki/Happy_End_(band)" title="Happy End (band)">Happy End</a> fusing rock with <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Japan" title="Music of Japan">Japanese music</a> in the early 1970s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-beatles_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-beatles-1">[1]</a></sup> J-pop was further defined by <a href="/wiki/New_wave_music" title="New wave music">new wave</a> groups in the late 1970s, particularly <a href="/wiki/Electronic_music" title="Electronic music">electronic</a> <a href="/wiki/Synthpop" title="Synthpop">synthpop</a> band <a href="/wiki/Yellow_Magic_Orchestra" title="Yellow Magic Orchestra">Yellow Magic Orchestra</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pop_rock" title="Pop rock">pop rock</a> band <a href="/wiki/Southern_All_Stars" title="Southern All Stars">Southern All Stars</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-whonejp_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-whonejp-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>Eventually, J-pop replaced <i><a href="/wiki/Kay%C5%8Dkyoku" title="Kayōkyoku">kayōkyoku</a></i> ("Lyric Singing Music", a term for Japanese pop music from the 1920s to the 1980s) in the Japanese music scene.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ctv_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-ctv-3">[3]</a></sup> The term was coined by the Japanese media to distinguish Japanese music from foreign music, and now refers to most Japanese popular music.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Form_and_definition">Form and definition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=J-pop&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Form and definition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Independent_music" title="Independent music">Independent music</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japanese_rock" title="Japanese rock">Japanese rock</a></div>
<p>The origin of modern J-pop is said to be Japanese-language <a href="/wiki/Rock_music" title="Rock music">rock music</a> inspired by the likes of <a href="/wiki/The_Beatles" title="The Beatles">The Beatles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-beatles_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-beatles-1">[1]</a></sup> Unlike the Japanese music genre called <i><a href="/wiki/Kay%C5%8Dkyoku" title="Kayōkyoku">kayōkyoku</a></i>, J-pop uses a special kind of pronunciation, which is similar to that of <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kobe_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-kobe-4">[4]</a></sup> One notable singer to do so is <a href="/wiki/Keisuke_Kuwata" title="Keisuke Kuwata">Keisuke Kuwata</a>, who pronounced the Japanese word <i>karada</i> ("body") as <i>kyerada</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kobe_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-kobe-4">[4]</a></sup> Additionally, unlike Western music, the <a href="/wiki/Major_second" title="Major second">major second</a> (<i>sol</i> and <i>la</i>) was usually not used in Japanese music, except <a href="/wiki/Art_music" title="Art music">art music</a>, before rock music became popular in Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matsuoka_Seigo_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Matsuoka_Seigo-5">[5]</a></sup> When the <a href="/wiki/Group_Sounds" title="Group Sounds">Group Sounds</a> genre, which was inspired by Western rock, became popular, Japanese pop music adopted the major second, which was used in the final sounds of The Beatles' song "<a href="/wiki/I_Want_to_Hold_Your_Hand" title="I Want to Hold Your Hand">I Want to Hold Your Hand</a>" and <a href="/wiki/The_Rolling_Stones" title="The Rolling Stones">The Rolling Stones</a>' song "<a href="/wiki/(I_Can%27t_Get_No)_Satisfaction" title="(I Can't Get No) Satisfaction">(I Can't Get No) Satisfaction</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matsuoka_Seigo_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Matsuoka_Seigo-5">[5]</a></sup> Although Japanese pop music changed from music based on Japanese <a href="/wiki/Pentatonic_scale" title="Pentatonic scale">pentatonic scale</a> and distortional <a href="/wiki/Tetrachord" title="Tetrachord">tetrachord</a> to the more occidental music over time, music that drew from the traditional Japanese singing style remained popular (such as that of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ringo_Shiina" title="Ringo Shiina">Ringo Shiina</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matsuoka_Seigo_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-Matsuoka_Seigo-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>At first, the term <i>J-pop</i> was used only for Western-style musicians in Japan, such as <a href="/wiki/Pizzicato_Five" title="Pizzicato Five">Pizzicato Five</a> and <a href="/wiki/Flipper%27s_Guitar" title="Flipper's Guitar">Flipper's Guitar</a>, just after Japanese radio station <a href="/wiki/J-Wave" title="J-Wave">J-Wave</a> was established.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ctv_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-ctv-3">[3]</a></sup> On the other hand, Mitsuhiro Hidaka of <a href="/wiki/AAA_(band)" title="AAA (band)">AAA</a> from <a href="/wiki/Avex_Trax" title="Avex Trax">Avex Trax</a> said that J-pop was originally derived from the <a href="/wiki/Eurobeat" title="Eurobeat">Eurobeat</a> genre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> However, the term became a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blanket_term" title="Blanket term">blanket term</a>, covering other music genres—such as the majority of Japanese rock music of the 1990s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ctv_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-ctv-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1990, the Japanese subsidiary of <a href="/wiki/Tower_Records" title="Tower Records">Tower Records</a> defined J-pop as all Japanese music belonging to the <a href="/wiki/Recording_Industry_Association_of_Japan" title="Recording Industry Association of Japan">Recording Industry Association of Japan</a> except Japanese <a href="/wiki/Independent_music" title="Independent music">independent music</a> (which they term "J-indie"); their stores began to use additional classifications, such as J-club, J-punk, <a href="/wiki/Japanese_hip_hop" title="Japanese hip hop">J-hip-hop</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japanese_reggae" title="Japanese reggae">J-reggae</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Music_in_Japanese_animation" title="Music in Japanese animation">J-anime</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Visual_kei" title="Visual kei">Visual kei</a> by 2008, after independent musicians started to release works via major labels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-excite_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-excite-7">[7]</a></sup> Ito Music City, a Japanese record store, adopted expanded classifications including Group Sounds, <a href="/wiki/Japanese_idol" title="Japanese idol">idol</a> of the 1970s–1980s, <a href="/wiki/Enka" title="Enka">enka</a>, <a href="/wiki/Folk_music" title="Folk music">folk</a> and established musicians of the 1970s–1980s, in addition to the main J-pop genres.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-excite_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-excite-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>Whereas rock musicians in Japan usually hate the term "pop", Taro Kato, a member of <a href="/wiki/Pop_punk" title="Pop punk">pop punk</a> band <a href="/wiki/Beat_Crusaders" title="Beat Crusaders">Beat Crusaders</a>, pointed out that the encoded <a href="/wiki/Pop_music" title="Pop music">pop music</a>, like <a href="/wiki/Pop_art" title="Pop art">pop art</a>, was catchier than "J-pop" and he also said that J-pop was the <i>pops</i> <span style="font-weight: normal">(<span class="t_nihongo_kanji" lang="ja">ポップス</span><span class="t_nihongo_comma" style="display:none">,</span> <i>poppusu</i><sup class="t_nihongo_help noprint"><a href="/wiki/Help:Installing_Japanese_character_sets" title="Help:Installing Japanese character sets"><span class="t_nihongo_icon" style="color: #00e; font: bold 80% sans-serif; text-decoration: none; padding: 0 .1em;">?</span></a></sup>)</span> music, memorable for its frequency of airplay, in an interview when the band completed their first full-length studio album under a major label, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/P.O.A.:_Pop_on_Arrival" title="P.O.A.: Pop on Arrival">P.O.A.: Pop on Arrival</a></i>, in 2005.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bikuru_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-bikuru-8">[8]</a></sup> Because the band did not want to perform J-pop music, their album featured the <a href="/wiki/1980s_in_music#Pop" title="1980s in music">1980s Pop</a> of <a href="/wiki/MTV" title="MTV">MTV</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bikuru_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-bikuru-8">[8]</a></sup> According to his fellow band member Toru Hidaka, the 1990s music that influenced him (such as <a href="/wiki/Nirvana_(band)" title="Nirvana (band)">Nirvana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hi-Standard" title="Hi-Standard">Hi-Standard</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Flipper%27s_Guitar" title="Flipper's Guitar">Flipper's Guitar</a>) was not listened to by fans of other music in Japan at that time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bikuru_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-bikuru-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>In contrast to this, although many Japanese rock musicians until the late 1980s disrespected the <i><a href="/wiki/Kay%C5%8Dkyoku" title="Kayōkyoku">kayōkyoku</a></i> music, many of Japanese rock bands of the 1990s—such as <a href="/wiki/Glay" title="Glay">Glay</a>—assimilated <i>kayōkyoku</i> into their music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ctv_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-ctv-3">[3]</a></sup> After the late 1980s, <a href="/wiki/Breakbeat" title="Breakbeat">breakbeat</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sampler_(musical_instrument)" title="Sampler (musical instrument)">samplers</a> also changed the Japanese music scene, where expert <a href="/wiki/Drummer" title="Drummer">drummers</a> had played good rhythm because <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Japanese_music" title="Traditional Japanese music">traditional Japanese music</a> did not have the rhythm based on rock or blues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ctv_3-4"><a href="#cite_note-ctv-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hide of <a href="/wiki/Greeeen" title="Greeeen">Greeeen</a> openly described their music genre as J-pop. He said, "I also love rock, hip hop and breakbeats, but my field is consistently J-pop. For example, hip hop musicians learn 'the culture of hip hop' when they begin their career. We are not like those musicians and we love the music as sounds very much. Those professional people may say 'What are you doing?' but I think that our musical style is cool after all. The good thing is good."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=J-pop&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1920s.E2.80.931960s:_Ry.C5.ABk.C5.8Dka">1920s–1960s: Ryūkōka</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=J-pop&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: 1920s–1960s: Ryūkōka">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Ry%C5%ABk%C5%8Dka" title="Ryūkōka">Ryūkōka</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kay%C5%8Dkyoku" title="Kayōkyoku">Kayōkyoku</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Japanese_jazz" title="Japanese jazz">Japanese jazz</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%E8%97%A4%E5%B1%B1%E4%B8%80%E9%83%8E.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1486" data-file-width="1162" height="192" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/%E8%97%A4%E5%B1%B1%E4%B8%80%E9%83%8E.jpg/150px-%E8%97%A4%E5%B1%B1%E4%B8%80%E9%83%8E.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/%E8%97%A4%E5%B1%B1%E4%B8%80%E9%83%8E.jpg/225px-%E8%97%A4%E5%B1%B1%E4%B8%80%E9%83%8E.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/%E8%97%A4%E5%B1%B1%E4%B8%80%E9%83%8E.jpg/300px-%E8%97%A4%E5%B1%B1%E4%B8%80%E9%83%8E.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%E8%97%A4%E5%B1%B1%E4%B8%80%E9%83%8E.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Ichiro Fujiyama, influential <i>ryūkōka</i> singer</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Japanese popular music, called <i><a href="/wiki/Ry%C5%ABk%C5%8Dka" title="Ryūkōka">ryūkōka</a></i> before being split into <i><a href="/wiki/Enka" title="Enka">enka</a></i> and <i>poppusu</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> has origins in the <a href="/wiki/Meiji_period" title="Meiji period">Meiji period</a>, but most Japanese scholars consider the <a href="/wiki/Taish%C5%8D_period" title="Taishō period">Taishō period</a> to be the actual starting point of <i>ryūkōka</i>, as it is the era in which the genre first gained nationwide popularity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yano33_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Yano33-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mini248_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mini248-12">[12]</a></sup> By the Taishō period, Western musical techniques and instruments, which had been <a href="/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_Meiji_Japan" title="Foreign relations of Meiji Japan">introduced to Japan in the Meiji period</a>, were widely used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mini248_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mini248-12">[12]</a></sup> Influenced by Western genres such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jazz_music" title="Jazz music">jazz</a> and <a href="/wiki/Blues" title="Blues">blues</a>, <i>ryūkōka</i> incorporated Western instruments such as the <a href="/wiki/Violin" title="Violin">violin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harmonica" title="Harmonica">harmonica</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Guitar" title="Guitar">guitar</a>. However, the melodies were often written according to the traditional Japanese <a href="/wiki/Pentatonic_scale" title="Pentatonic scale">pentatonic scale</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yano33_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-Yano33-11">[11]</a></sup> In the 1930s, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ichiro_Fujiyama" title="Ichiro Fujiyama">Ichiro Fujiyama</a> released popular songs with his <a href="/wiki/Tenor" title="Tenor">tenor</a> voice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Fujiyama sang songs with a lower volume than <a href="/wiki/Opera" title="Opera">opera</a> through the <a href="/wiki/Microphone" title="Microphone">microphone</a> (the technique is sometimes called <i><a href="/wiki/Crooner" title="Crooner">crooning</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (September 2010)">dubious</span></a> <span class="metadata">– <a href="/wiki/Talk:J-pop#Dubious" title="Talk:J-pop">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Jazz musician <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ryoichi_Hattori" title="Ryoichi Hattori">Ryoichi Hattori</a> attempted to produce Japanese native music which had a "flavor" of <a href="/wiki/Blues" title="Blues">blues</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> He composed <a href="/wiki/Noriko_Awaya" title="Noriko Awaya">Noriko Awaya</a>'s hit song "Wakare no Blues" (lit. "Farewell Blues").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ryoichihattori_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-ryoichihattori-16">[16]</a></sup> Awaya became a famous popular singer and was called "Queen of Blues" in Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Due to pressure from the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Japanese_Army" title="Imperial Japanese Army">Imperial Army</a> during the war, the performance of jazz music was temporarily halted in Japan. Hattori, who stayed in <a href="/wiki/Shanghai" title="Shanghai">Shanghai</a> at the end of the war, produced hit songs such as <a href="/wiki/Shizuko_Kasagi" title="Shizuko Kasagi">Shizuko Kasagi</a>'s "Tokyo Boogie-Woogie" and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ichiro_Fujiyama" title="Ichiro Fujiyama">Ichiro Fujiyama</a>'s "Aoi Sanmyaku" (lit. "Blue Mountain Range").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ryoichihattori_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-ryoichihattori-16">[16]</a></sup> Hattori later became known as the "Father of Japanese <i>poppusu</i>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ryoichihattori_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-ryoichihattori-16">[16]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> soldiers—who were <a href="/wiki/Occupation_of_Japan" title="Occupation of Japan">occupying Japan</a> at the time—and the <a href="/wiki/Far_East_Network" title="Far East Network">Far East Network</a> introduced a number of new musical styles to the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jazzkissa_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-jazzkissa-18">[18]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Boogie-woogie" title="Boogie-woogie">Boogie-woogie</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mambo_(music)" title="Mambo (music)">Mambo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Blues" title="Blues">Blues</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Country_music" title="Country music">Country music</a> were performed by Japanese musicians for the American troops. <a href="/wiki/Chiemi_Eri" title="Chiemi Eri">Chiemi Eri</a>'s cover song "<a href="/wiki/Tennessee_Waltz" title="Tennessee Waltz">Tennessee Waltz</a>" (1952), <a href="/wiki/Hibari_Misora" title="Hibari Misora">Hibari Misora</a>'s "Omatsuri Mambo" (1952), and <a href="/wiki/Izumi_Yukimura" title="Izumi Yukimura">Izumi Yukimura</a>'s cover song "<a href="/wiki/Till_I_Waltz_Again_with_You" title="Till I Waltz Again with You">Till I Waltz Again with You</a>" (1953) also became popular. Foreign musicians and groups, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/JATP" title="JATP">JATP</a> and <a href="/wiki/Louis_Armstrong" title="Louis Armstrong">Louis Armstrong</a>, visited Japan to perform. In the mid-1950s, "Jazz Kissa" <span style="font-weight: normal">(<span class="t_nihongo_kanji" lang="ja">ジャズ喫茶</span><span class="t_nihongo_comma" style="display:none">,</span> <i>Jazu Kissa, literally "Jazz cafe"</i><sup class="t_nihongo_help noprint"><a href="/wiki/Help:Installing_Japanese_character_sets" title="Help:Installing Japanese character sets"><span class="t_nihongo_icon" style="color: #00e; font: bold 80% sans-serif; text-decoration: none; padding: 0 .1em;">?</span></a></sup>)</span> became a popular venue for live jazz music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jazzkissa_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-jazzkissa-18">[18]</a></sup> Jazz had a large impact on Japanese <i>poppusu</i>, though "authentic" jazz did not become the mainstream genre of music in Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> In the late 1950s and early 1960s, Japanese pop was polarized between urban <i><a href="/wiki/Kay%C5%8Dkyoku" title="Kayōkyoku">kayō</a></i> and modern <i>enka</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1960s:_Origin_of_modern_style">1960s: Origin of modern style</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=J-pop&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 1960s: Origin of modern style">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kyu_Sakamoto_1964.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="295" data-file-width="447" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Kyu_Sakamoto_1964.png/250px-Kyu_Sakamoto_1964.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Kyu_Sakamoto_1964.png/375px-Kyu_Sakamoto_1964.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fa/Kyu_Sakamoto_1964.png 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kyu_Sakamoto_1964.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Kyu_Sakamoto" title="Kyu Sakamoto">Kyu Sakamoto</a> (left) in 1964. His song "<a href="/wiki/Sukiyaki_(song)" title="Sukiyaki (song)">Sukiyaki</a>" became the first Japanese song to reach number-one on the <a href="/wiki/Billboard_Hot_100" title="Billboard Hot 100"><i>Billboard</i> Hot 100</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Rokabir.C4.AB_Boom_and_Wasei_pops">Rokabirī Boom and Wasei pops</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=J-pop&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Rokabirī Boom and Wasei pops">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>During the 1950s and 60s, many Kayōkyoku groups and singers gained experience performing on US military bases in Japan. Around the same time, <a href="/wiki/Yakuza" title="Yakuza">Yakuza</a> manager <a href="/wiki/Kazuo_Taoka" title="Kazuo Taoka">Kazuo Taoka</a> reorganized the concert touring industry by treating the performers as professionals. Many of these performers later became key participants in the J-pop genre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1956, Japan's <a href="/wiki/Rock_and_roll" title="Rock and roll">rock and roll</a> craze began, due to the country music group known as Kosaka Kazuya and the Wagon Masters; their rendition of <a href="/wiki/Elvis_Presley" title="Elvis Presley">Elvis Presley</a>'s song "<a href="/wiki/Heartbreak_Hotel" title="Heartbreak Hotel">Heartbreak Hotel</a>" helped to fuel the trend. The music was called "<a href="/wiki/Rockabilly" title="Rockabilly">rockabilly</a>" (or <i>rokabirī</i>) by the Japanese media.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Performers learned to play the music and translate the lyrics of popular American songs, resulting in the birth of <i>Cover Pops</i> <span style="font-weight: normal">(<span class="t_nihongo_kanji" lang="ja">カヴァーポップス</span><span class="t_nihongo_comma" style="display:none">,</span> <i>Kavā poppusu</i><sup class="t_nihongo_help noprint"><a href="/wiki/Help:Installing_Japanese_character_sets" title="Help:Installing Japanese character sets"><span class="t_nihongo_icon" style="color: #00e; font: bold 80% sans-serif; text-decoration: none; padding: 0 .1em;">?</span></a></sup>)</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-showahibari_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-showahibari-23">[23]</a></sup> The rockabilly movement would reach its peak when 45,000 people saw the performances by Japanese singers at the first <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Nichigeki&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Nichigeki (page does not exist)">Nichigeki</a> Western Carnival in one week of February 1958.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Kyu_Sakamoto" title="Kyu Sakamoto">Kyu Sakamoto</a>, a fan of Elvis, made his stage début as a member of the band <a href="/wiki/The_Drifters_(Japanese_band)" title="The Drifters (Japanese band)">The Drifters</a> at the Nichigeki Western Carnival in 1958.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> His 1961 song "Ue wo Muite Arukō" ("Let's Look Up and Walk"), known in other parts of the world as "<a href="/wiki/Sukiyaki_(song)" title="Sukiyaki (song)">Sukiyaki</a>", was released to the United States in 1963. It was the first Japanese song to reach the Number One position in the United States, spending four weeks in <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cash_Box" title="Cash Box">Cash Box</a></i> and three weeks in <i><a href="/wiki/Billboard_(magazine)" title="Billboard (magazine)">Billboard</a></i>. It also received a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gold_record" title="Gold record">gold record</a> for selling one million copies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NippopSAKAMOTO_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-NippopSAKAMOTO-26">[26]</a></sup> During this period, female duo <a href="/wiki/The_Peanuts" title="The Peanuts">The Peanuts</a> also became popular, singing a song in the movie <i><a href="/wiki/Mothra_(film)" title="Mothra (film)">Mothra</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> Their songs, such as "Furimukanaide" ("Don't Turn Around") were later covered by <a href="/wiki/Candies_(group)" title="Candies (group)">Candies</a> on their album <i>Candy Label</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Artists like Kyu Sakamoto and The Peanuts were called <i>Wasei Pops</i> <span style="font-weight: normal">(<span class="t_nihongo_kanji" lang="ja">和製ポップス</span><span class="t_nihongo_comma" style="display:none">,</span> <i>Wasei poppusu</i><sup class="t_nihongo_help noprint"><a href="/wiki/Help:Installing_Japanese_character_sets" title="Help:Installing Japanese character sets"><span class="t_nihongo_icon" style="color: #00e; font: bold 80% sans-serif; text-decoration: none; padding: 0 .1em;">?</span></a></sup>, "Japan-made pop")</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-showahibari_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-showahibari-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>After frequently changing members, <a href="/wiki/Chosuke_Ikariya" title="Chosuke Ikariya">Chosuke Ikariya</a> re-formed The Drifters in 1964 under the same name. At a Beatles concert in 1966, they acted as curtain raisers, but the audience generally objected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arai_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-arai-30">[30]</a></sup> Eventually, The Drifters became popular in Japan, releasing "Zundoko-Bushi" ("Echoic word tune") in 1969.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arai_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-arai-30">[30]</a></sup> Along with <i>enka</i> singer <a href="/wiki/Keiko_Fuji" title="Keiko Fuji">Keiko Fuji</a>, they won "the award for mass popularity" at the <a href="/wiki/12th_Japan_Record_Awards" title="12th Japan Record Awards">12th Japan Record Awards</a> in 1970.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Keiko Fuji's 1970 album <i>Shinjuku no Onna/'Enka no Hoshi' Fuji Keiko no Subete</i> ("Woman in Shinjuku/'Star of <i>Enka</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span> All of Keiko Fuji") established an all-time record in the history of the Japanese <a href="/wiki/Oricon" title="Oricon">Oricon</a> chart by staying in the Number One spot for 20 consecutive weeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1000numberone_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-1000numberone-32">[32]</a></sup> The Drifters later came to be known as television personalities and invited <a href="/wiki/Japanese_idol" title="Japanese idol">idols</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Momoe_Yamaguchi" title="Momoe Yamaguchi">Momoe Yamaguchi</a> and Candies to their television program.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arai_30-2"><a href="#cite_note-arai-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ereki_Boom_and_Group_Sounds">Ereki Boom and Group Sounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=J-pop&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Ereki Boom and Group Sounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Group_Sounds" title="Group Sounds">Group Sounds</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nippon_Budokan_2010.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1800" data-file-width="3578" height="101" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Nippon_Budokan_2010.jpg/200px-Nippon_Budokan_2010.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Nippon_Budokan_2010.jpg/300px-Nippon_Budokan_2010.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Nippon_Budokan_2010.jpg/400px-Nippon_Budokan_2010.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nippon_Budokan_2010.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Nippon Budokan, legendary place for Japanese musicians</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/The_Ventures" title="The Ventures">The Ventures</a> visited Japan in 1962, causing the widespread embrace of the <a href="/wiki/Electric_guitar" title="Electric guitar">electric guitar</a> called the "<i>Ereki</i> Boom".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Y%C5%ABz%C5%8D_Kayama" title="Yūzō Kayama">Yūzō Kayama</a> and <a href="/wiki/Takeshi_Terauchi" title="Takeshi Terauchi">Takeshi Terauchi</a> became famous players of electric guitar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ventures_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-ventures-34">[34]</a></sup> In 1966, <a href="/wiki/The_Beatles" title="The Beatles">the Beatles</a> came to Japan and sang their songs at the <a href="/wiki/Nippon_Budokan" title="Nippon Budokan">Nippon Budokan</a>, becoming the first <a href="/wiki/Rock_music" title="Rock music">rock music</a> band to perform a concert there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> The public believed that the Beatles would cause <a href="/wiki/Juvenile_delinquency" title="Juvenile delinquency">juvenile delinquency</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-beatles_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-beatles-1">[1]</a></sup> The Japanese government deployed <a href="/wiki/Riot_police" title="Riot police">riot police</a> against young rock fans at the Nippon Budokan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Lennon" title="John Lennon">John Lennon</a> felt that they were not well regarded in Japan, but <a href="/wiki/Beatlemania" title="Beatlemania">Beatlemania</a> has never really died there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> The Beatles inspired Japanese bands, creating the <a href="/wiki/Group_Sounds" title="Group Sounds">Group Sounds</a> genre in Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-beatles_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-beatles-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>Most Japanese musicians felt that they could not sing rock in <a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese</a>, so the popularity of Japanese rock gradually declined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-beatles_1-4"><a href="#cite_note-beatles-1">[1]</a></sup> As a result, there were debates such as "Should we sing rock music in Japanese?" and "Should we sing in English?" between <a href="/wiki/Happy_End_(band)" title="Happy End (band)">Happy End</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Y%C5%ABya_Uchida_(singer/actor)" title="Yūya Uchida (singer/actor)">Yuya Uchida</a> about Japanese rock music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> This confrontation was called "Japanese-language rock controversy" <span style="font-weight: normal">(<span class="t_nihongo_kanji" lang="ja">日本語ロック論争</span><span class="t_nihongo_comma" style="display:none">,</span> <i>Nihongo Rokku Ronsō</i><sup class="t_nihongo_help noprint"><a href="/wiki/Help:Installing_Japanese_character_sets" title="Help:Installing Japanese character sets"><span class="t_nihongo_icon" style="color: #00e; font: bold 80% sans-serif; text-decoration: none; padding: 0 .1em;">?</span></a></sup>)</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> Happy End proved that rock music could be sung in Japanese, and one theory holds that their music became one of the origins of modern J-pop.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-beatles_1-5"><a href="#cite_note-beatles-1">[1]</a></sup> The Beatles also inspired <a href="/wiki/Eikichi_Yazawa" title="Eikichi Yazawa">Eikichi Yazawa</a>, who grew up in an underprivileged family, his father dying when he was a child.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Keisuke_Kuwata" title="Keisuke Kuwata">Keisuke Kuwata</a>, who grew up in a dual-income family, was influenced by the Beatles through his older sister, then an avid fan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Y%C5%8Dsui_Inoue" title="Yōsui Inoue">Yōsui Inoue</a> was also a fan of The Beatles, but he said that his music style was not particularly related to them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> After Happy End disbanded in 1973, <a href="/wiki/Haruomi_Hosono" title="Haruomi Hosono">Haruomi Hosono</a>, a former member, began a solo career and later formed <a href="/wiki/Yellow_Magic_Orchestra" title="Yellow Magic Orchestra">Yellow Magic Orchestra</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1970s:_Development_of_.22New_Music.22">1970s: Development of "New Music"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=J-pop&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title='Edit section: 1970s: Development of "New Music"'>edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="F.C5.8Dku_and_New_Music"><i>Fōku</i> and New Music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=J-pop&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Fōku and New Music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In the early 1960s, some Japanese music became influenced by the <a href="/wiki/American_folk_music_revival" title="American folk music revival">American folk music revival</a>; this was called <i>fōku</i> <span style="font-weight: normal">(<span class="t_nihongo_kanji" lang="ja">フォーク</span><sup class="t_nihongo_help noprint"><a href="/wiki/Help:Installing_Japanese_character_sets" title="Help:Installing Japanese character sets"><span class="t_nihongo_icon" style="color: #00e; font: bold 80% sans-serif; text-decoration: none; padding: 0 .1em;">?</span></a></sup>, "folk")</span>, although the genre of music was mostly covers of original songs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> In the late 1960s, <a href="/wiki/The_Folk_Crusaders" title="The Folk Crusaders">The Folk Crusaders</a> became famous and the underground music around that time became called <i>fōku</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> As with <i>enka</i>, Japanese <i>fōku</i> singers <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Wataru_Takada&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Wataru Takada (page does not exist)">Wataru Takada</a> performed social satires.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the early 1970s, the emphasis shifted from <i>fōku</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s simple songs with a single guitar accompaniment to more complex musical arrangements known as <b>New Music</b> <span style="font-weight: normal">(<span class="t_nihongo_kanji" lang="ja">ニューミュージック</span><span class="t_nihongo_comma" style="display:none">,</span> <i>nyū myūjikku</i><sup class="t_nihongo_help noprint"><a href="/wiki/Help:Installing_Japanese_character_sets" title="Help:Installing Japanese character sets"><span class="t_nihongo_icon" style="color: #00e; font: bold 80% sans-serif; text-decoration: none; padding: 0 .1em;">?</span></a></sup>)</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newmusic_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-newmusic-47">[47]</a></sup> Instead of social messages, the songs focused on more personal messages, such as love. In 1972, singer-songwriter <a href="/wiki/Takuro_Yoshida" title="Takuro Yoshida">Takuro Yoshida</a> produced a hit song "Kekkon Shiyouyo" ("Let's marry") without decent television promotion, though fans of <i>fōku</i> music became very angry because his music seemed to be a mersh <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="not a word (February 2013)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-asahi_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-asahi-48">[48]</a></sup> The highest-selling single of the year was the <i>enka</i> song by <a href="/wiki/Shiro_Miya" title="Shiro Miya">Shiro Miya</a> and the Pinkara Trio, "<a href="/wiki/Onna_no_Michi" title="Onna no Michi">Onna no Michi</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-asahi_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-asahi-48">[48]</a></sup> The song eventually sold over 3.25 million copies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smap_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-smap-49">[49]</a></sup> On December 1, 1973, <a href="/wiki/Y%C5%8Dsui_Inoue" title="Yōsui Inoue">Yōsui Inoue</a> released the album <i><a href="/wiki/K%C5%8Dri_no_Sekai" title="Kōri no Sekai">Kōri no Sekai</a></i>, which topped the Oricon charts and remained in Top 10 for 113 weeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> It spent 13 consecutive weeks in the number-one spot, and eventually established a still-standing record of a total 35 weeks at the number-one position on the Oricon charts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1000numberone_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-1000numberone-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Yumi_Matsutoya" title="Yumi Matsutoya">Yumi Matsutoya</a>, formerly known by her maiden name Yumi Arai, also became a notable singer/songwriter during this period In October 1975, she released a single "Ano Hi ni Kaeritai" ("I want to return to that day"), making it her first number-one single on the Oricon charts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Miyuki_Nakajima" title="Miyuki Nakajima">Miyuki Nakajima</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amii_Ozaki" title="Amii Ozaki">Amii Ozaki</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Junko_Yagami" title="Junko Yagami">Junko Yagami</a> were also popular singer-songwriters during this period. At first, only Yumi Matsutoya was commonly called a New Music artist, but the concept of Japanese <i>fōku</i> music changed around that time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-53">[53]</a></sup> In 1979, <a href="/wiki/Chage_and_Aska" title="Chage and Aska">Chage and Aska</a> made their debut, and folk band <a href="/wiki/Off_Course" title="Off Course">Off Course</a> (with singer <a href="/wiki/Kazumasa_Oda" title="Kazumasa Oda">Kazumasa Oda</a>) released a hit song "Sayonara" ("Good-bye").</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Emergence_of_Japanese_rock_and_electronic_music">Emergence of Japanese rock and electronic music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=J-pop&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Emergence of Japanese rock and electronic music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japanese_rock" title="Japanese rock">Japanese rock</a> and <a href="/wiki/Synthpop" title="Synthpop">Synthpop</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Rock_music" title="Rock music">Rock music</a> remained a relatively <a href="/wiki/Underground_music" title="Underground music">underground music</a> genre in the early 1970s in Japan,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newmusic_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-newmusic-47">[47]</a></sup> though <a href="/wiki/Happy_End_(band)" title="Happy End (band)">Happy End</a> managed to gain mainstream success fusing rock with traditional <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Japan" title="Music of Japan">Japanese music</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-beatles_1-6"><a href="#cite_note-beatles-1">[1]</a></sup> Several Japanese musicians began experimenting with <a href="/wiki/Electronic_music" title="Electronic music">electronic music</a>, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electronic_rock" title="Electronic rock">electronic rock</a>. The most notable was the internationally renowned <a href="/wiki/Isao_Tomita" title="Isao Tomita">Isao Tomita</a>, whose 1972 album <i>Electric Samurai: Switched on Rock</i> featured electronic <a href="/wiki/Synthesizer" title="Synthesizer">synthesizer</a> renditions of contemporary rock and <a href="/wiki/Pop_music" title="Pop music">pop songs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jenkins_2007_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins_2007-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jenkins_2007_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins_2007-54">[54]</a></sup> Other early examples of electronic rock records include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inoue_Yousui" title="Inoue Yousui">Inoue Yousui</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Folk_rock" title="Folk rock">folk rock</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pop_rock" title="Pop rock">pop rock</a> album <i>Ice World</i> (1973) and <a href="/wiki/Osamu_Kitajima" title="Osamu Kitajima">Osamu Kitajima</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Progressive_rock" title="Progressive rock">progressive</a> <a href="/wiki/Psychedelic_rock" title="Psychedelic rock">psychedelic rock</a> album <i><a href="/wiki/Benzaiten" title="Benzaiten">Benzaiten</a></i> (1974), both of which involved contributions from Haruomi Hosono,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> who later started the electronic music group "Yellow Magic Band" (later known as Yellow Magic Orchestra) in 1977.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-discogs_paraiso_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-discogs_paraiso-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1978, <a href="/wiki/Eikichi_Yazawa" title="Eikichi Yazawa">Eikichi Yazawa</a>'s rock single "Jikan yo Tomare" ("Time, Stop") became a smash hit that sold over 639,000 copies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> He is regarded as one of the pioneers of Japanese rock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> He sought worldwide success, and in 1980 he signed a contract with the Warner Pioneer record company and moved to the West Coast of the United States. He recorded the albums <i>Yazawa</i>, <i>It's Just Rock n' Roll</i>, and <i>Flash in Japan</i>, all of which were released worldwide, but were not very commercially successful. <a href="/wiki/Keisuke_Kuwata" title="Keisuke Kuwata">Keisuke Kuwata</a> formed the rock band <a href="/wiki/Southern_All_Stars" title="Southern All Stars">Southern All Stars</a> (SAS), which made their debut in 1978. Southern All Stars remains very popular in Japan today.</p>
<p>In the same year, <a href="/wiki/Yellow_Magic_Orchestra" title="Yellow Magic Orchestra">Yellow Magic Orchestra</a> (YMO) also made their official debut with their <a href="/wiki/Yellow_Magic_Orchestra_(album)" title="Yellow Magic Orchestra (album)">self-titled album</a>. The band, whose members were <a href="/wiki/Haruomi_Hosono" title="Haruomi Hosono">Haruomi Hosono</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yukihiro_Takahashi" title="Yukihiro Takahashi">Yukihiro Takahashi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ryuichi_Sakamoto" title="Ryuichi Sakamoto">Ryuichi Sakamoto</a>, developed <a href="/wiki/Electropop" title="Electropop">electropop</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> or <a href="/wiki/Techno" title="Techno">technopop</a> as it is known in Japan,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sarasota_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-sarasota-61">[61]</a></sup> in addition to pioneering <a href="/wiki/Synthpop" title="Synthpop">synthpop</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electro_music" title="Electro music">electro music</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wire_1996_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-wire_1996-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-broughton_2007_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-broughton_2007-63">[63]</a></sup> Their 1979 album <i><a href="/wiki/Solid_State_Survivor" title="Solid State Survivor">Solid State Survivor</a></i> reached number one on the <a href="/wiki/Oricon" title="Oricon">Oricon</a> charts in July 1980,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> and went on to sell two million records worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hardy_1987_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hardy_1987-65">[65]</a></sup> At around the same time, the YMO albums <i>Solid State Survivor</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/X%E2%88%9EMultiplies" title="X∞Multiplies">X∞Multiplies</a></i> held both the top two spots on the <a href="/wiki/Oricon" title="Oricon">Oricon</a> charts for seven consecutive weeks, making YMO the only band in Japanese chart history to achieve this feat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> Young fans of their music during this period became known as the "YMO Generation" <span style="font-weight: normal">(<span class="t_nihongo_kanji" lang="ja">YMO世代</span><span class="t_nihongo_comma" style="display:none">,</span> <i>YMO sedai</i><sup class="t_nihongo_help noprint"><a href="/wiki/Help:Installing_Japanese_character_sets" title="Help:Installing Japanese character sets"><span class="t_nihongo_icon" style="color: #00e; font: bold 80% sans-serif; text-decoration: none; padding: 0 .1em;">?</span></a></sup>)</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> YMO had a significant impact on Japanese pop music, which started becoming increasingly dominated by <a href="/wiki/Electronic_music" title="Electronic music">electronic music</a> due to their influence,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-loubet_couroux_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-loubet_couroux-69">[69]</a></sup> and they had an equally large impact on electronic music across the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fidelity_1993_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-fidelity_1993-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allmusic_ymo_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-allmusic_ymo-71">[71]</a></sup> Southern All Stars and Yellow Magic Orchestra symbolized the end of New Music and paved the way for the emergence of the J-pop genre in the 1980s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-whonejp_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-whonejp-2">[2]</a></sup> Both bands, SAS and YMO, would later be ranked at the top of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HMV_Group" title="HMV Group">HMV Japan</a>'s list of top 100 Japanese musicians of all time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1980s:_Fusion_with_.22kay.C5.8Dkyoku.22">1980s: Fusion with "kayōkyoku"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=J-pop&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title='Edit section: 1980s: Fusion with "kayōkyoku"'>edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="City_Pop">City Pop</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=J-pop&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: City Pop">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Shibuya-kei" title="Shibuya-kei">Shibuya-kei</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:212px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Keigo_Oyamada_and_Ryuichi_Sakamoto.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2415" data-file-width="3372" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Keigo_Oyamada_and_Ryuichi_Sakamoto.jpg/210px-Keigo_Oyamada_and_Ryuichi_Sakamoto.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Keigo_Oyamada_and_Ryuichi_Sakamoto.jpg/315px-Keigo_Oyamada_and_Ryuichi_Sakamoto.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Keigo_Oyamada_and_Ryuichi_Sakamoto.jpg/420px-Keigo_Oyamada_and_Ryuichi_Sakamoto.jpg 2x" width="210"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
